Introduction

A brief overview of this book’s contents

This is the third time I’ve accidentally written a book. I began writing a section about the 2025 World Series for my first book (On Moonlight: a film analysis, autobiography, & holistic societal critique) that grew far beyond my initial plans for it; I reached the inescapable conclusion that I was writing a third book when I realized it had grown to 70,000 words with no end in sight. (My second book, Aequilībrium harmoniae: a mathematical analysis of scales, modes, & the circle of fifths, is a lengthy, technically detailed treatise on music theory; Aequilībrium harmoniae is Latin for both The Balance of Harmony and The Harmony of Balance.)

Honor lūdī is paraphrased from one of Dodgers manager Dave Roberts’ favorite sayings, “The game honors you.” It means both honor of the game and the game of honor, owing to the unusual grammar of Latin’s genitive case. I intend both meanings. An exact Latin translation of “the game honors you” would be lūdus te honōrat.

I cover numerous topics in this book, including but hardly limited to:

  • Babe Ruth wasn’t the only player to be successful as both a hitter and a pitcher before Shōhei Ohtani.

    • Contrary to popular misconception, Ruth was a two-way player in 1918 and 1919.
    • Ruth arguably wasn’t even his era’s most accomplished two-way player: Hall of Famers Leon Day, Martín Dihigo, Bullet Joe Rogan, and even Jimmie Foxx were significantly better pitchers.
    • You’ll meet such two-way players as:
      • The eponymous founder of a sporting goods giant whose win percentage will never be bettered
      • The tragically overworked player who threw every pitch of the 1877 Louisville Grays’ season
      • The man who “could play every position on the team”, but died of typhoid fever at age 25
      • The Hall of Famer who pitched like Walter Johnson, hit like Ty Cobb, and fielded like Willie Mays
      • The Hall of Famer with over 500 home runs and a career ERA below 2.00 (it’s not who you think)
  • The 2025 World Series’ finale is objectively the greatest baseball game in history, with more dramatic shifts in win chance than any game had ever featured before.
  • The impact of players like Jackie Robinson, Roberto Clemente, Lip Pike, Hank Aaron, Hank Greenberg, Sandy Koufax, Ichiro Suzuki, Hideo Nomo, Hideki Matsui, Yoshinobu Yamamoto, and Shōhei Ohtani on our culture.
  • Ted Williams calling out the Hall of Fame in his induction speech directly precipitated the induction of Negro League legends like Satchel Paige, Josh Gibson, Oscar Charleston, and Buck Leonard.
  • Jackie Robinson wasn’t Major League Baseball’s first, second, or even third Black player, and there were numerous other Black players in nineteenth-century minor leagues, many of whom are now Hall of Famers.
  • Major League Baseball’s first (and worst) commissioner very likely delayed its racial integration by a decade.
  • Shoeless Joe Jackson not only didn’t throw 1919’s World Series but performed at a level even Ernie Clement likely wishes he’d matched in 2025’s.
  • Segregation wasn’t just racist; it damaged baseball’s play standards, since great white hitters (e.g., Ty Cobb, Rogers Hornsby, Shoeless Joe Jackson, Ted Williams) never faced great Black pitchers (e.g., Bill Foster, Bullet Joe Rogan, Martín Dihigo, Smokey Joe Williams), and great Black hitters (e.g., Josh Gibson, Oscar Charleston, Tetelo Vargas, Buck Leonard) never faced great white pitchers (e.g., Walter Johnson, Christy Mathewson, Carl Hubbell, Grover Cleveland Alexander). All those players instead faced many opponents who’d have been minor leaguers without segregation, which inflated their statistics. Corollaries of this include:
    • No one bats .400 anymore not because hitters have gotten worse, but because pitchers have gotten better. Tony Gwynn’s .394 batting average in 1994 is the most impressive in NL or AL history.
    • Likewise, sub-1.50 ERAs disappeared due to hitters getting better (and the pitching mound being lowered by five inches in 1969). Pedro Martínez’s 1.74 ERA in 2000 is the most impressive in MLB history.
  • Baseball just had the longest gap in back-to-back championships in the history of the Big Four American sports leagues (MLB, NBA, NFL, NHL), and it’s the only one without a salary cap.
  • The Los Angeles Dodgers are successful less because of their high spending and more because they’re MLB’s best-run franchise. (The Mets spend more and didn’t even make the playoffs in 2025.)

Along the way, you’ll also learn about such colorful characters as:

  • Two pitchers who came within one out of completing perfect games, only for what the umpires themselves later admitted were blown calls to spoil them
  • One of the All-American Girls’ Professional Baseball League’s stars, a two-way player who to this day remains the only top-level professional athlete to have thrown two perfect games
  • The Japanese pitcher who was pulled one inning away from throwing his second perfect game in a row, only to have his team lose the game in extras
  • The pitcher who threw a no-hitter while tripping on LSD
  • The pitcher who, after an injury that otherwise might’ve ended his career, taught himself to throw left-handed so he could continue playing the outfield that season
  • The Negro league star who holds all-time records for batting average, slugging percentage, and OPS, and has been said to have hit more home runs than Barry Bonds by no less a source than Barry Bonds

On my congenital bias against the Toronto Blue Jays

With one exception, I mostly don’t follow professional sports. I followed the 1990s Chicago Bulls: Michael Jordan, Scottie Pippen, Dennis Rodman, Toni Kukoč, Luc Longley, and Robert Parish were poetry in motion. Until the 2024-2025 Los Angeles Dodgers rolled along, they felt like the only equivalent I’d ever see of the 1920s Yankees’ “Murderer’s Row” in any sport: a candidate for the greatest player of all time and several others who’d be other teams’ star players. I knew I was witnessing something special at the time, and I hardly find it surprising that six members of that team (Jordan, Pippen, Rodman, Kukoč, Parish, and coach Phil Jackson) are now Hall of Famers.

But I think I was mostly a fan of that particular lineup, not basketball itself; after the 1997-98 season, Jordan retired the first time, and Pippen, Rodman, and Longley were traded to other teams (Parish had already retired after 1996-97, while Kukoč was traded midway through the 1999-2000 season. Parish played 1611 games, which remains an NBA record, though LeBron James is about a season and a half away from surpassing it). The magic had gone, and I stopped following the sport. (It doesn’t help that, being 5’5”, I never had the slightest hope of developing any aptitude for it myself.)

My first and greatest love among sports is and probably always will be baseball. Reasons for this include:

  • The head games between pitchers and hitters resemble nothing in any other sport I know.
  • A truly well-turned play possesses a grace I find difficult to describe in words.
  • Baseball analysis, with its vast array of esoteric statistics, is all but tailor-made for neurodivergent people.

I briefly wanted to be a baseball pitcher, but I never could learn to throw the ball fast enough,⁽¹⁾ which I’ll put down to lacking the competitive drive truly needed to succeed in team sports.⁽²⁾ I can occasionally be induced to be competitive in games, but my friends have to stir it out of me.

I must preface all the following remarks by noting that I was born in Atlanta, Georgia. My understanding is that geneticists have isolated this as the key factor in congenital biases against both Game 7 of the 1991 World Series (in which the Minnesota Twins defeated the Atlanta Braves 1–0 in ten innings) and the Toronto Blue Jays (who defeated the Braves in 1992’s World Series). I will admit that objectively, 1991’s Game 7 should qualify as one of the best of all time, but I’m still not over it, and it will never, ever top my “greatest Game 7s of all time” list. (I’ve at least cooled down enough to place it in my top ten, even though I still hate the outcome.)

Moreover, even if I weren’t an Atlanta native, familial loyalty would still obligate me to be a Braves fan: their star pitcher Chris Sale is my second cousin. Sale, a nine-time All-Star, has won a World Series ring, a Gold Glove, a Cy Young Award, and pitching’s Triple Crown. He also reached the milestones of 2,000 and 2,500 strikeouts with fewer innings pitched than any pitcher in history, unseating Hall of Famers Pedro Martinez and Randy Johnson respectively. He shares the Major League record of three immaculate innings (nine pitches, three strikeouts) with Hall of Famer Sandy Koufax and future Hall of Famer (and current Blue Jays starter) Max Scherzer; he’s also the only pitcher ever to throw two immaculate innings in one season (2019-05-08 and 2019-06-05). In at least one season, he’s had MLB’s best win count (2024), win-loss ratio (2024), ERA (2024), ERA+ (2024), FIP (2017, 2024), strikeouts per nine innings pitched (2015, 2017, 2024), and home runs per nine innings pitched (2024), and its highest totals for strikeouts (2017), complete games (2016), and innings pitched (2017). He’ll likely be elected to the Hall of Fame in his first year of eligibility, and unless an injury derails the rest of his career, he’ll likely be one of the last pitchers to reach the 3,000-strikeout milestone.

(I’ll have a rundown of several crucial baseball stats after this section.)

For some reason, though, I’ve mostly forgiven the Twins for 1991. I haven’t forgiven the Jays for 1992. I’ve made many painful, even embarrassing revelations about my childhood and adolescence elsewhere in my autobiography. This easily dwarfs them all. Atlanta remains one of my favorite cities in the world, and writing anything positive about any incarnation of any Toronto Blue Jays team not only pains me to my core but literally, not figuratively, feels like an act of treason against my birthplace. In case anyone thinks I’m being hyperbolic or facetious here, I promise that I’m not. A nagging part of my subconscious protests that I’m betraying a core part of myself every time I compliment the Blue Jays or one of their players, and I can’t help wondering whether that part of me is right. Nonetheless, honesty compels me to be objective.

I’m not even sure it’s the franchise, per se, that I dislike. If anything, I gained a considerable amount of overall respect for the 2025 team for its World Series performance (aside from George Springer, whom I doubt I’ll ever forgive his role in the 2017 Houston Astros’ sign-stealing). The team’s relievers made a particularly classy gesture after what was described at the time as a “family emergency” forced Dodgers reliever Alex Vesia, whose uniform number is 51, to drop out of the World Series. (This ultimately was revealed to be his infant daughter’s illness; sadly, she passed away on October 26.) I’ll let Kiké Hernández describe:

“Yeah, I didn’t notice until … after [Chris] Bassitt struck me out, and then I was looking up at the board to see the replay, and that’s when I saw that he had 51 [on his hat], and instead of being mad that I struck out, I was kind of going back to the dugout thinking, ‘Did Bassitt play with Vesia at some point?’ And then, after the game, I saw that everybody had them. For those guys to do that, it’s incredible. They’re trying to win a Series, but they understand that … life is bigger than baseball, and baseball’s just a game. For them to do that, with … where we were at with the stakes? Hats off to them, and I want them to know that … regardless of what happens tonight, we appreciate what they did.”

And the majority of Jays fans have handled a loss that must rival how 1991 felt to us Braves fans with unimaginable grace and class. As usual, a small but vocal minority of fans are ruining everyone else’s reputation.

The reason the Twins escaped my long-time loathing, but the Jays didn’t, is largely due to the behavior of one particularly insufferable Canadian classmate in 1992 who seemed determined to teach everyone a valuable lesson by proving that not all stereotypes are always accurate: for instance, not all Canadians are polite. This was 33 years ago, so I’m not even going to attempt to recount memories of any particular incidents, which I’m sure are horrendously inaccurate. I’ll just say I didn’t know the word Backpfeifengesicht (literally, “a face in need of a slap”: of course the Germans would have a word for this – not just an expression, a single word!) in 1992, but if I had, I’m sure I’d have used it to describe him. To be clear, I don’t condone unprovoked violence, but I was in fifth grade at the time,⁽³⁾ and few things are more infuriating than a sore winner.

As I said, the majority of Jays fans this year really did nothing to earn my ire. However, I only saw signs personally attacking a specific player from one Major League Baseball franchise’s fans this season, and in case you need a hint which franchise that was, here it is: that franchise is not based in the United States. I’d still be more willing to forgive even this if they weren’t targeting one of the nicest, humblest, hardest-working athletes in the history of the sport, a legitimate contender for the greatest to ever play it, and a member of one of this country’s most vulnerable populations besides.

I’m referring, of course, to Shōhei Ohtani (Japanese: 大谷 翔平; Hepburn: Ōtani Shōhei), baseball’s nearest equivalent to Michael Jordan in my lifetime, an all-rounder of historically unprecedented caliber. Before I explain why, though, it’s probably a good idea to provide context for the statistics I’ll be referring to throughout this book.

Introduction to baseball statistics

Wins Above Replacement: What is it good for?

Wins Above Replacement (WAR) is a statistic tracking a player’s effectiveness over that of a replacement player, i.e., a player just brought up from the minor leagues. I won’t bore readers with a description of the exact formula, especially since formulae vary. I’ll use the stats from Baseball Reference, to which I also routinely link.

There are actually three main forms of WAR, although most players not named “Shōhei Ohtani” have had at most two since the National League adopted the designated hitter:

Αἱ τρεῖς κεφᾰ́λαιαι κᾰτηγορῐ́αι τοῦ ΠΟΛΈΜΟΥ
Hai treîs kephắlaiai kătēgorĭ́ai toû POLÉMOU
The three main categories of WAR
NameAcronymWhat it’s good forWho it covers
Defensive WARdWARFieldingEveryone but designated hitters
Offensive WARoWARHitting, baserunningHitters, two-way players
Pitching WARpWARPitchingPitchers, two-way players

Having already wandered so far into the trees that I’m afraid of losing sight of the forest, I’ve combined dWAR and oWAR into non-pitching WAR (nWAR, pronounced like noir in film noir). All MLB players have nWAR.

WAR is cumulative, but it’s often helpful to evaluate it over specific time spans to compare players’ consistency (especially if their career lengths varied). For this purpose, I’ve created the following metrics:

  • NWFS, or nWAR per full season of 162 games. (Not to be confused with NSFW.)
  • PWSS, or pWAR per starter’s season of 200 innings pitched.

Back to top · Table of contents · My portfolio · Contact me · Website index

Pitching stats

Reliability

  • Win–loss records and win percentage are one of the oldest pitcher efficacy metrics…and one of the worst.

    Bob Hendley gave up only a hit, a walk, and an unearned run on 1965-09-09. He lost. Why? His counterpart, the Dodgers’ Sandy Koufax, allowed no runs, no hits, and no walks, and the Dodgers made no errors (a perfect game). Was Hendley’s outing bad? No, of course not. The Cubs just couldn’t hit Koufax. That wasn’t their fault: Hall of Famer Willie Stargell compared trying to hit Koufax to “eating soup with a fork”.

    This is an extreme example, but it goes to show these metrics’ limitations. Decisions (individual wins and losses) are almost useless for relievers, who rarely enter games with blank slates. If I enter a game my team is winning, I’m only credited with a win if I give up at least enough runs to tie the game, then my teammates regain it. Likewise, if I enter one we’re losing, I can only get a loss if my teammates score at least enough to tie the game, then I give up enough runs to set us behind. Effectively, our situation when I enter greatly affects my odds of earning wins or losses, and our manager controls when I enter; I don’t. This is why other stats exist for relievers. Arguably, a high decision count being a red flag for a reliever is the only thing that keeps reliever decisions from being trivia.

    Comparing starters’ record and win% to their other stats can provide perhaps surprising insights not on the starters, but on their teammates and opponents. We can conclude that a pitcher with a tied or losing record, low WHIP, and high ERA+ is actually an incredible pitcher who likely suffers from either:

    1. being on the 2025 Pittsburgh Pirates a team whose batters couldn’t hit the broad side of a barn, or
    2. not finishing games and being on the 2025 Los Angeles Dodgers a team with a terrible bullpen.

  • Several similar statistics are meant to track reliever effectiveness. Since 1975, MLB rules have credited a save as occurring when a pitcher:

    1. is still pitching when their team wins;
    2. is not credited with the victory;
    3. is credited with at least ⅓ IP; and either:
      1. enters with a lead of three runs or fewer and throws at least one inning;
      2. enters with the potential tying run either on base, at bat, or on deck; or
      3. throws at least three innings.

    In a potential save opportunity, a blown save involves giving up the game-tying run (whether or not the runner was already on base). A reliever in the same situation who records at least one out without blowing the save or finishing the game is credited with a hold and is not credited with a save opportunity. Save percentage is calculated by dividing a reliever’s saves by their save opportunities.

    These statistics have come in for some criticism; Hall of Fame relievers Rich “Goose” Gossage and Rollie Fingers each blew over 100 saves but were still extraordinarily effective overall. It’s arguably better to use one’s best relievers strategically in close games than to uniformly use them for every game’s final inning. Sabermetric analysis has clearly demonstrated, however, that the most effective reliever is the previous game’s javelin-throwing starter; no such pitcher has ever lost a World Series game.

  • Baseball sabermetrician and FiveThirtyEight founder Nate Silver has proposed a replacement statistic, the goose egg, named “to honor (or troll) Gossage”, its all-time leader despite currently being only 27th in career saves. A reliever gets a goose egg each time:

    1. the inning is the seventh or later;
    2. at the time of the first batter the pitcher faced that inning, either:
      1. the game was tied,
      2. their team led by two runs or fewer, or
      3. the opposing team had the potential tying run on base or at the plate;
    3. in the course of the inning, both:
      1. no earned or unearned runs are charged to the pitcher, and
      2. no inherited runners score while the pitcher is in the game; and
    4. the pitcher either:
      1. records three outs (one inning pitched), or
      2. records at least one out, and the sum of outs plus inherited baserunners when the pitcher entered was greater than two.

    Relievers are saddled with broken eggs when:

    1. At least one earned run is charged to the reliever;
    2. The reliever could’ve gotten a goose egg by recording enough outs;
    3. The reliever does not close out the win for his team.

    And they’re credited with mehs if a run charged to another pitcher or an unearned run scores while they’re on the mound. Silver argues that:

    “[M]anagers are trying to maximize the number of saves for their closer, as opposed to the number of wins for their team. They’re managing to a stat and playing worse baseball as a result. … [A save] doesn’t give a pitcher any additional reward for pitching multiple innings — even though two clutch innings pitched in relief are roughly twice as valuable as one. And a pitcher doesn’t get a save for pitching in a tie game, even though it’s one of the highest-leverage situations.”

    He also argues that goose eggs and broken eggs correlate much more closely (0.78) to the much more complex statistic Win Probability Added than saves and blown saves do (0.50).

    Goose Wins Above Replacement is to goose eggs as pitching WAR is to wins. (Unfortunately, Dave Brockie [RIP] is not MLB’s leader in GWAR.) Stu Miller had the best single-season GWAR in 1965, with 79 goose eggs to 7 broken eggs for 7.5 GWAR. His traditional stats looked good enough at 14–7, 1.89 ERA, 24 saves, 119⅓ IP, and 4.2 pWAR, but he was almost unhittable in high-leverage situations. Lifetime save leader Mariano Rivera also leads in lifetime GWAR with 54.6, but his best season according to GWAR was not 2004 (53 saves, 47 goose eggs, 4 broken eggs, 2.09 ERA, and 232 ERA+ in 78⅔, 4.2 pWAR, 5.4 GWAR) IP, but 1996 (5 saves, 54 goose eggs, 6 broken eggs, 1.94 ERA, 240 ERA+, 107⅓ IP, 5.0 pWAR, 6.6 GWAR).

    Yoshinobu Yamamoto got three goose eggs out of three opportunities in World Series Game 7 this year. Will Klein got four out of four in Game 3. In and of itself, that feels like sufficient proof of this statistic’s value.

Back to top · Table of contents · My portfolio · Contact me · Website index

Run prevention

  • ERA, or Earned Run Average, is a traditional metric of how often a pitcher gives up earned runs, or runs that aren’t the result of fielding errors. A lower number is better. The formula for calculating it is simple: Earned Runs × 9 ÷ Innings Pitched. It has several limitations, but it’s still widely used, partly because it’s easy to understand and calculate. Also, unlike ERA+, it’s possible to calculate for postseason games.

    Ed Walsh, the all-time career leader, has a 1.816 career ERA. He also pitched exclusively in what’s called the “dead-ball era” because it was extremely hitter-unfriendly. The dead-ball era ended after 1919, coincidentally Babe Ruth’s last season with the Boston Red Sox.

    The modern leader, Mariano Rivera, retired in 2013 with a 2.209 career ERA. Rivera was primarily a reliever, meaning that he worked in smaller spans. Relievers tend to have lower ERAs. Clayton Kershaw, who just retired, has the lowest career ERA among modern starters, with 2.534. The increase in ERA over the years is less due to a decline in pitching quality and more due to rule changes and an increase in hitting quality.

  • ERA+ has overtaken ERA as one of the most important indicators of a pitcher’s effectiveness in recent years, though it has a few weaknesses (most notably, it isn’t calculated for postseason games, and it’s also more complicated to explain). Unlike ERA, it’s adjusted to factors such as the overall league average ERA and how hitter-friendly the ballpark is – thus, runs allowed at the Colorado Rockies’ Coors Field, which is notoriously hitter-friendly due to being over a mile above sea level, will count less against a pitcher’s ERA+ than runs allowed at the Miami Marlins’ LoanDepot Park, which is, unsurprisingly, at sea level. Perhaps the most important difference from ERA, though, is that a higher ERA+ number is better. This stat’s career leaders are:

    1. Mariano Rivera (205, active 1995–2013)
    2. Bill Foster (164, active 1923–37)
    3. Bullet Rogan (161, active 1915–38)
    4. Clayton Kershaw (154, active 2008–25)
    5. Pedro Martínez (154, active 1992–2009)
    6. Jacob deGrom (151, active 2014–)
    7. Jim Devlin (150, active 1875–1877)
    8. Satchel Paige (150, active 1927–65 – that is not a typo)

    The fact that this list spans baseball’s entire history suggests it measures a pitcher’s relative dominance much more reliably than ERA does. Also, although not every great pitcher in history ranks this high on this list (it’s missing Sandy Koufax, Greg Maddux, Bob Gibson, Randy Johnson, and several other immortals), every pitcher on this list was great. (Even Devlin, who burned briefly but brightly – no one has ever duplicated his feat of throwing literally every pitch his team threw that year, and no one ever will.)

  • FIP stands for Fielding-Independent Pitching; it’s another adjusted version of ERA that measures a pitcher’s effectiveness at striking out batters and preventing home runs and walks, set in such a way that the league average FIP and league average ERA are the same. Unlike ERA+, FIP is still calculated for the postseason. The FIP records look pretty similar to the ERA records; Ed Walsh tops it with 2.018.

Back to top · Table of contents · My portfolio · Contact me · Website index

Hit and walk ratios

  • WHIP stands for Walks plus Hits per Inning Pitched. Again, a lower number is better. This has overtaken ERA as of the most important measures of pitching effectiveness in recent years. A single-season WHIP of below 1 is generally considered elite. The all-time career leader, Addie Joss, had 0.9678 WHIP over 2327 IP and pitched exclusively in the dead-ball era, but second-place Jacob deGrom is the modern-era leader with 0.9859 WHIP over 1539⅔ IP. This is another stat where you can pick any of the top ten all-time leaders and find a phenomenal pitcher.
  • H/9IP or H9 stand for Hits per Nine Innings Pitched. Obviously, lower ratios are better. Nine innings are, of course, the length of a regulation ballgame. Nolan Ryan, the Ryan Express himself, is the all-time leader with 6.5553 H9 over 5386 IP. Jacob deGrom is a close second and leads active players with 6.8567.
  • BB/9IP or BB9 (not to be confused with BB8) stands for Bases on Balls per Nine Innings Pitched. Again, lower numbers are better. Hall of Famer Candy Cummings, most famous for inventing inventor the curveball, leads this stat with 0.4371 BB9 in 2149⅔ IP. Unsurprisingly, he last pitched in 1877. Only one active pitcher even cracks this stat’s top 50: Miles Mikolas, in 49th with 1.7679. That’s almost four times Cummings’ record, which, like Devlin’s, will never be broken. That’s not speculation; it’s a cold, hard fact.
  • SO/9IP or SO9 stand for Strikeouts per Nine Innings Pitched. Higher numbers are more desirable here. Blake Snell recently became this stat’s all-time career leader with 11.1917, surpassing Chris Sale’s 11.1377. Since modern baseball puts so much more emphasis on strikeouts, this stat’s top ten all played this century, and only two are inactive: Stephen Strasburg (eighth place with 10.5490) last pitched in 2022, and the Big Unit himself, Randy Johnson (sixth place with 10.6098), last pitched in the halcyon days of 2009.
  • SO/BB stands for Strikeouts per Base on Balls. Again, higher numbers are better. deGrom is unsurprisingly the all-time leader in this stat, with an absolutely filthy 5.3652, though shouts-out to Chris Sale for being not far behind with 5.2957.

Back to top · Table of contents · My portfolio · Contact me · Website index

Feats of Strength

  • A complete game means a pitcher started the game and threw every pitch to every opposing batter. This is only somewhat dependent on the pitcher’s effectiveness, since reliever use occurs at a manager’s discretion, and other factors also affect whether a pitcher receives a complete game credit. A rain-shortened game or a seven-inning game that is part of a double-header still counts as a complete game; an extra-inning game that a pitcher leaves after nine innings does not.

    Complete games have become extraordinarily rare. In 1904, 87.6% of games were complete games; in 2024, 0.6% were. This is largely due to increasingly strict adherence to pitch counts, to the extent that it was considered extraordinarily unusual when Yoshinobu Yamamoto threw 96 pitches in Game 6 of this year’s World Series, then another 34 in Game 7. (This is nothing for Yoshi, who’d thrown up to 140 pitches in NPB games, and who throws javelins for practice.) It has been speculated that this may lead to a decrease in reliance on pitch counts, since Yamamoto has been healthy over a career in which he has thrown a lot, while despite the modern obsession with pitch counts, pitchers are suffering more injuries than ever before.

  • A shutout is a complete game in which a pitcher does not allow a single run, earned or otherwise. A game in which multiple pitchers combine to throw a shutout is not officially credited as a shutout, although it is often informally called a combined shutout.

  • A no-hitter is a game of at least nine innings in which a pitcher does not allow a single hit. The Venn diagram between shutouts and no-hitters is a large circle mostly overlapping with a smaller circle: no-hitters can allow runs if batters reach on errors or pitchers walk too many batters. Most no-hitters are complete games, although MLB’s most recent no-hitter was a combined no-hitter thrown on 2024-09-04 by Shōta Imanaga, Nate Pearson, and Porter Hodge. Only three postseason no-hitters have ever been thrown:

    1. Don Larsen (1956-10-08), which was also a perfect game.
    2. Roy Halladay (2010-10-06).
    3. Cristian Javier, Bryan Abreu, Rafael Montero, & Ryan Pressly’s combined no-hitter (2022-11-02).

  • A perfect game is a game of nine innings or more in which no opposing batter safely reaches base. Thus, no walk, hit, hit batsman, catcher’s interference, fielder’s obstruction, fielding error, or uncaught third strike may allow any batter to reach base. As a result of a 1991 rule changes, a previously perfect game spoiled in extra innings does not count. Only twenty-four perfect games have officially been pitched in MLB history. No one has ever pitched more than one, and Don Larsen’s perfect game in the 1956 World Series is the only one in postseason history. Domingo Germán threw the most recent perfect game on 2023-06-28.

    A perfect game, by definition, is a no-hitter, but not necessarily a shutout, although so far, they all have been. The “ghost runner” in extra-inning regular-season games does not spoil a perfect game, even if he advances due to such occurrences as sacrifice flies or fielding errors, so a pitcher could at least theoretically pitch a perfect game he still loses. However, there has never been an official extra-inning perfect game, so this has never happened. (I can almost guarantee that it will if any Los Angeles Angels pitcher ever manages to carry a perfect game into extra innings. If any team can find a way to lose a game in which their players do something incredible and historically unprecedented, it’s the Angels. I list two cases of games that were perfect until extra innings below. I also list unofficial perfect games the umpires themselves subsequently admitted they spoiled with bad calls below.)

    Here are all twenty-four official MLB perfect games. FS stands for Final Score, PC for Pitch Count, and K for Strikeouts. Hall of Famers are italicized.

Baseball Reference says Barker threw 102 pitches in his perfect game; Baseball Almanac says 103. Likewise, BR says Browning threw 101 and BA says 102. Finally, BR says Martínez threw 96 and BA says 95. One of these days, if I feel especially bored, I may review all three videos and see which is correct.

The Rangers and Angels are the only teams to throw perfect games against each other (in the same ballpark, at that). The Yankees threw the only consecutive perfect games (strangely, in back-to-back years, and both by pitchers named David), while the Dodgers and Rays had consecutive perfect games thrown against them (the latter also in back-to-back years).

Honorable mentions:

  • On 1908-07-04 (BR, SABR), the New York Giants’ George Leroy “Hooks” Wiltse lost a perfect game against the Philadelphia Phillies on a 2–2 count in a perfect game when he hit the twenty-seventh batter, opposing pitcher George McQuillan. He ulimately won the game 1–0 in ten innings. However, umpire Cy Rigler later admitted that he had miscalled a strike, meaning that Wiltse’s game should’ve been perfect. Wiltse later recalled, “Every time I saw Charlie Rigler after that he gave me a cigar. He admits [the disputed ball] was one of the pitches he missed.” Wiltse’s game remains arguably the greatest extra-inning no-hitter in MLB history; three others went into extras, and they all lasted ten innings.
  • The 1991 rule change retroactively removed perfect status from a 1959-05-26 game (BR, BA, SABR, WP) in which the Pittsburgh Pirates’ Harvey Haddix threw 12⅔ perfect innings against the Milwaukee Braves before subsequently losing it to an error by Pirates third baseman Don Hoak. Pittsburgh then went on to lose the game when Joe Adcock hit an apparent home run (later ruled a ground-rule double due to a rare baserunning mistake by Hank Aaron, who did not see the ball clear the first fence). Haddix handled the decertification of his perfect game a lot more gracefully than I would’ve, saying only, “It’s OK. I know what I did.” Milwaukee’s Bob Buhl, at a banquet for the game’s 30th anniversary, subsequently admitted to having stolen signals from catcher Smoky Burgess and relayed them to Milwaukee’s hitters with a towel. Aaron was the Braves’ only batter who refused to take the signals (which gives his own response to the Houston Astros’ 2017 sign-stealing scandal, “I think whoever did that should be out of baseball for the rest of their lives,” a lot more force). Despite this, Adcock had Milwaukee’s only hit; Aaron had been intentionally walked. Arguably the only greater effort by a losing pitcher in MLB history was Bob Hendley’s in Koufax’s perfect game: Hendley gave up one hit and no earned runs and still got the L.
  • On 1995-06-03 (BR, BA, SABR), the Montreal Expos’ Pedro Martínez (no relation to Dennis) gave up a double to the San Diego Padres’ Bip Roberts in the bottom of the tenth inning; Montreal manager Felipe Alou then brought in reliever Mel Rojas. After a wild pitch, Rojas retired the next three batters, and Montreal won the game 1–0. Martínez displayed a lot more aplomb than I would’ve in his shoes, saying, “We went on to win, but according to the rulebook, I could not be given credit for a perfect game, even though I had gone nine perfect innings. Reporters afterward told me that the last person to lose a perfect game in extra innings was Harvey Haddix. I really wasn’t that upset about losing a perfect game according to a technicality. Everyone with the Expos thought I received some vindication.” Alou responded, “This was the best answer Pedro could give… I’m not surprised that he threw this kind of game.”

  • Unofficially, Armando Galarraga retired twenty-eight consecutive batters on 2010-06-02 (SABR, WP). How? Umpire Jim Joyce ruled that the Cleveland Indians’ Jason Donald had safely reached first. Galarraga handled this far more gracefully than I would’ve, saying Joyce “probably feels more bad than me. Nobody’s perfect. Everybody’s human. I understand. I give the guy a lot of credit for saying, ‘I need to talk to you.’ You don’t see an umpire tell you that after a game. I gave him a hug.”

    Hall of Fame closer Mariano Rivera said:

    “It happened to the best umpire we have in our game. The best. And a perfect gentleman… It’s a shame for both of them, for the pitcher and for the umpire. But I’m telling you he is the best baseball has, and a great guy. It’s just a shame.”

    Milt Pappas, who lost a 1972-09-02 bid for a perfect game (BR, SABR) when umpire Bruce Froemming called two borderline pitches to 27th batter Larry Stahl balls, told Galarraga:

    “I feel for you. There have been only 20 perfect games in the history of baseball. The umpire situation was the same one I had; they blew it. At least I had the satisfaction of getting a no-hitter. You don’t. I feel for you; you pitched a tremendous game. At least you have the satisfaction of the umpire saying he was sorry. But that doesn't help your situation as far as a perfect game.”

    (Pappas subsequently retired the twenty-eighth batter, preserving the no-hitter. He also reacted a lot more like I suspect I would’ve in his shoes: he was initially furious; then, after the game, he publicly gave Froemming the benefit of the doubt; but over the decades, Froemming’s refusal to second-guess his own calls increasingly infuriated Pappas.)

    Both Galarraga and Joyce received praise for their handling of the situation from sources up to and including President Barack Obama, who said their conduct “showed something about sportsmanship that you don’t see enough of in America today.” Some two weeks after the game, Joyce was voted MLB’s best umpire with 53% of votes; one player said:

    “The sad thing…is, Jim Joyce is seriously one of the best umpires around… He always calls it fair, so players love him. Everyone makes mistakes, and it’s terrible that this happened to him.”

    Joyce and Galarraga have both called for Galarraga’s game to be retroactively certified as a perfect game, but thus far, baseball commissioners Bud Selig and Rob Manfred have refused calls to do so. However, it was likely a major factor in MLB’s subsequent implementation of the instant replay system.

Perfect game win-loss records for franchises with at least two (using cumulative scores as tiebreakers):

  1. New York Yankees: 4–0 (score: 23–0)
  2. Philadelphia Phillies: 2–0 (score: 7–0)
  3. Cleveland Naps/Indians/Guardians: 2–0 (score: 4–0)
  4. Chicago White Sox: 3–1 (score: 11–1)
  5. Philadelphia/Oakland Athletics: 2–2 (score: 8–14)
  6. Texas Rangers: 1–1 (score: 4–1)
  7. California/Los Angeles Angels: 1–1 (score: 1–4)
  8. Brooklyn/Los Angeles Dodgers: 1–3 (score: 1–5)
  9. Minnesota Twins: 0–2 (score: 0–8)
  10. Tampa Bay Rays: 0–3 (score: 0–10)

9+ inning perfect games in other top-level professional sports leagues:

  • Nippon Professional Baseball has had sixteen official perfect games in its history:
    1. 1950-07-28: Hideo Fujimoto (藤本 英雄, Yomiuri Giants) vs. Nishi-Nippon Pirates, 4–0, 7 K
    2. 1955-06-19: Fumio Takechi (武智 文雄, Kintetsu Pearls) vs. Daiei Stars, 1–0, 6 K
    3. 1956-09-19: Yoshitomo Miyaji (宮地 惟友, Kokutetsu Swallows) vs. Hiroshima Carp, 6–0, 3 K
    4. 1957-08-21: Masaichi Kaneda (金田 正一, Kokutetsu Swallows) vs. Chunichi Dragons, 1–0, 10 K
    5. 1958-07-19: Sadao Nishimura (西村 貞朗, Nishitetsu Lions) vs. Toei Flyers, 1–0, 6 K
    6. 1960-08-11: Gentarō Shimada (島田 源太郎, Taiyo Whales) vs. Osaka Tigers, 1–0, 3 K
    7. 1961-06-20: Yoshimi Moritaki (森滝 義巳, Koktetsu Swallows) vs. Chunichi Dragons, 1–0, 4 K
    8. 1966-05-01: Yoshirō Sasaki (佐々木 吉郎, Taiyo Whales) vs. Hiroshima Carp, 1–0, 7 K
    9. 1966-05-12: Tsutomu Tanaka (田中 勉, Nishitetsu Lions) vs. Nankai Hawks, 2–0, 7 K
    10. 1968-09-14: Yoshiro Sotokoba (外木場 義郎, Hiroshima Toyo Carp) vs. Taiyo Whales, 2–0, 16 K
    11. 1970-10-06: Koichiro Sasaki (佐々木 宏一郎, Kintetsu Buffaloes) vs. Nankai Hawks, 3–0, 4 K
    12. 1971-08-21: Yoshimasa Takahashi (高橋 善正, Toei Flyers) vs. Nishitetsu Lions, 4–0, 1 K
    13. 1973-08-10: Soroku Yagisawa (八木沢 荘六, Lotte Orions) vs. Taiheiyo Club Lions, 1–0, 6 K
    14. 1978-08-31: Yutaro Imai (今井 雄太郎, Hankyu Braves) vs. Lotte Orions, 5–0, 3 K
    15. 1994-05-18: Hiromi Makihara (槙原 寛己, Yomiuri Giants) vs. Hiroshima Toyo Carp, 6–0, 7 K
    16. 2022-04-10: Rōki Sasaki (佐々木 朗希, Chiba Lotte Marines) vs. Orix Buffaloes, 6–0, 19 K

    Notes:

    • As far as I know, all three Sasakis are unrelated.
    • In Makihara’s game, the Giants dropped a foul fly ball, which was scored as an error, but Makihara went on to retire the batter, so he kept his perfect game.
    • Rōki was the youngest pitcher to throw a perfect game in NPB history; he is also the only pitcher I know of to have fanned more than two-thirds of the batters he faced in a perfect game. At one point he struck out 13 straight batters, which is an all-time NPB record. He was also perfect in the next eight innings he threw on 2022-04-16, but he was removed after having thrown 102 pitches in what at the time was a scoreless tie (Chiba lost the game 1–0 in extra innings).
    • The World Baseball Softball Confederation also recognizes a 2007-11-01 Japan Series-clinching 1–0, seven-strikeout victory by the Chunichi Dragons’ Daisuke Yamai (山井 大介) and Hitoke Iwase (岩瀬 仁紀) over the Hokkaido Nippon-Ham Fighters as a combined perfect game; it is the only combined perfect game in top-level pro baseball history to span a regulation nine innings.
  • The Chinese Professional Baseball League has had one, thrown by Ryan Verdugo on 2018-10-07.

  • The All-American Girls Professional Baseball League had five perfect games in its history (1943–1954):

    1. 1944-07-29: Annabelle “Lefty” Lee (Minneapolis Millerettes) vs. the Kenosha Comets
      • Later named Annabelle Harmon; her nephew, MLB pitcher Bill “Spaceman” Lee, called her “the best athlete in our family, including me” and credited her with teaching him to play.
    2. 1945-07-06 Carolyn Morris (Rockford Peaches) vs. the Fort Wayne Daisies
    3. 1947-08-18: Doris “Sammye” Sams (Muskegon Lassies) vs. the Fort Wayne Daisies
      • Sams, a superb two-way player, had a career .290 batting average (sixth-best in league history) and .368 slugging%; she also set a league record in 1952 with twelve home runs. Unsurprisingly, she was twice selected as its Player of the Year.
    4. 1951-07-21: Jean Faut (South Bend Blue Sox) vs. the Rockford Peaches
    5. 1953-09-03: Jean Faut (South Bend Blue Sox) vs. the Muskegon Lassies
      • Faut, also an excellent two-way player (.243 average, .299 slugging%), was the only pro baseball player ever to throw two perfect games; she ended her career with a 140–64 record and 1.23 ERA, the lowest for any AAGPBL pitcher with at least 500 IP. She was second in wins only to Helen Nicol Fox (163), and third on its career strikeouts list with 913. She was also a two-time Player of the Year; she and Sams were the only players so honored.

  • A Maddux, named for Hall of Famer Greg Maddux, is a complete-game shutout thrown in fewer than 100 pitches. Because 1988 was the first year of pitch count tracking, they are not tracked before then, although some games have since been determined to qualify for the accomplishment.

    • Greg Maddux is the eponym because he has the record, with thirteen in his twenty-three-year career. His closest runner-up, Zane Smith, threw seven. Maddux and Smith also share the single-season record:

      • Zane Smith: 1991-05-03, 1991-05-29, 1991-09-21
      • Greg Maddux: 1998-04-15, 1998-06-07, 1998-08-06
    • Nathan Eovaldi (2025-04-01), Tarik Skubal (2025-05-25), and Sonny Gray (2025-06-27) threw the only Madduxes of 2025. Skubal set a record for most strikeouts by a pitcher who threw a Maddux, with 13.
    • Jon Lieber’s 78-pitch Maddux on (2001-05-24) is the low mark for pitch count since 1988. As for all-time low marks, see below.
      • Maddux threw a 78-pitch complete-game victory (1997-07-22), but allowed a single earned run; his low mark for pitch count in his own Madduxes was 84 (1997-07-02).
    • Hall of Famer Roy Halladay threw the only known extra-inning Maddux on 2003-09-06.
    • Orel Hersheiser threw the only Maddux in the playoffs since 1988, a 94-pitch shutout on 1988-10-12.

    • Thirteen Madduxes since 1988 were no-hitters; at least five (in bold and italics) were also perfect games. Baseball Almanac has also retroactively found five pre-1988 perfect games to have been Madduxes.

      1. Addie Joss (1908-10-02, 74 pitches)
      2. Charlie Robertson (1922-06-21, 90 pitches)
      3. Don Larsen (1956-10-08, 97 pitches)
      4. Jim Bunning (1964-06-21, 90 pitches)
      5. Mike Witt (1984-09-30, 94 pitches)
      6. Dennis Martínez (1991-07-28, 96 pitches)
      7. Kevin Gross (1992-08-17, 99 pitches)
      8. Chris Bosio (1993-04-22, 97 pitches)
      9. Kenny Rogers (1994-07-28, 98 pitches)
      10. Kevin Brown (1997-06-10, 98 pitches)
      11. David Cone (1999-07-18, 88 pitches)
      12. Derek Lowe (2002-04-27, 97 pitches)
      13. Philip Humber (2012-04-21, 96 pitches)
      14. Henderson Alvarez III (2013-09-29, 99 pitches)
      15. Edinson Vólquez (2017-06-03, 98 pitches)
      16. James Paxton (2018-05-08, 99 pitches)
      17. Domingo Germán (2023-06-28, 99 pitches)
      18. Framber Valdez (2023-08-01, 93 pitches)
      • Joss’ game is a singular accomplishment, standing out not only as a perfect game but also for having the lowest pitch count of any known complete game. His opponent, Hall of Famer Ed Walsh, was also excellent, fanning 15 batters but giving up one run; thus, it can be argued that Walsh technically holds the record for most strikeouts in a Maddux, even though he didn’t pitch it himself. Sadly, it’s far too early for any audio of this legendary pitcher’s duel to survive, much less video. (Ed Walsh has the third-lowest career WHIP in history, with 0.9996. Joss has the lowest in history, with 0.9678. Walsh has the lowest career ERA in history, with 1.816. Joss has the second-lowest in history, with 1.887. Walsh has the lowest career FIP in history, with 2.018. Joss has the fourth-lowest in history, with 2.249. In short, if I had the ability to time-travel back to a single game to film it, it would, without hesitation, be this one. Koufax’s perfect game would be the second, but at least we have Vin Scully’s call of its final inning.)
      • Larsen’s perfect game is the only postseason no-hitter known to be a Maddux. Halladay’s no-hitter was five pitches over the limit, and the 2022 combined no-hitter was forty-two over.
      • Cone’s game is the clear standout since 1988, not just for being a perfect game, but also for having this list’s lowest pitch count in that era and tying Vólquez’ game for its highest strikeout total (10). Additionally, Larsen threw its ceremonial first pitch.
      • Darryl Kile gets an honorable mention for an 83-pitch no-hitter (1993-09-08) that lost shutout status to an unearned run on a wild pitch.

      • Maddux’s low mark for hits in his own Madduxes was 2 on 1995-08-20.
    • As with complete games, the number of Madduxes thrown has steadily decreased. Every year from 1988 to 1993 featured at least 13; no year since 1993 has featured more than 13. 1988’s 26 Madduxes were the peak; 2018, 2022, and 2023 share the fewest Madduxes among full seasons, with 2 each. The strike-shortened 1994 still contained six Madduxes. 2015 was the last season to exceed that total.
    • I apologize for the exhaustive level of detail, but if I were forced at gunpoint to create a rotation of five starters in any year from 1960–2010, I’d pick Sandy Koufax ca. 1963, Bob Gibson ca. 1968, Greg Maddux ca. 1995, Pedro Martínez ca. 2000, and Randy Johnson ca. 2001. In short, this accomplishment’s eponym is one of my favorite pitchers of all time, so it’s little surprise that it’s one of my favorites.

(Intro to hitting stats TK)

Why Shōhei Ohtani may be the best baseball player of all time

Members of the 50–50 club: Shōhei Ohtani (end of list)

Let’s start with one of Ohtani’s less eye-catching (but still record-setting) accomplishments. Before 2024, no player who had hit fifty or more home runs had stolen more than twenty-four bases in the same season. Willie Mays (1955) and Alex Rodriguez (2007) shared the record. Similarly, Ronald Acuña Jr. had the high count for home runs in a season with at least fifty stolen bases: seventy-three stolen bases, forty-one home runs.

There’s a lot of reasons for this, starting with the obvious one that stealing bases is an achievement of speed, while hitting home runs is an achievement of power. A perhaps slightly less obvious one is that these two goals are mutually exclusive. Every single home run a player hits is a chance they will not get to steal a base.

Before the game on 2024-09-19, Ohtani had forty-eight home runs and forty-nine stolen bases that season.

When it ended, he didn’t have fifty home runs, nor did he have fifty stolen bases.

He had fifty-one home runs and fifty-one stolen bases.

In all, he went six for six, with five extra-base hits, seventeen total bases, and ten RBI. The Dodgers clinched their playoff berth with a 20–4 victory. Miami Marlins manager Skip Schumaker also deserves credit for the respect he showed both Ohtani and the game of baseball. In the top of the seventh inning, Ohtani was four for four with a home run, two doubles, and two stolen bases, and Los Angeles led 11–3. Schumaker was asked about intentionally walking Ohtani. He responded, “Fuck that. Too much respect for this guy for that shit to happen.”

After the game, he called the idea of an intentional walk:

“a bad move… karma-wise, baseball god-wise. You go after him and see if you can get him out. Out of respect for the game, we’re gonna go after him. He hit the home runs; that’s just part of the deal.”

(File this quote away in your mind – we’ll be returning to it later.)

He also called Ohtani “the most talented player” he’d ever seen (high praise indeed from a former teammate of future Hall of Famer Albert Pujols) and said Ohtani was:

“doing things that I’ve never seen done before…. If he has a couple more of these peak years, he might be the best ever to play the game.… It was a good day for baseball, a bad day for the Marlins.”

The Marlins’ audience clearly understood they were witnessing history as well: even though their team was being blown out, they responded to his accomplishments with enthusiastic applause. (This is hardly unprecedented; visiting pitchers often get standing ovations from home crowds after throwing perfect games.)

In that game, Ohtani became the first:

  • MLB player to hit bat in ten runs and steal a base in the same game.
  • MLB player to hit three home runs and steal two bases in the same game.
  • MLB player to bat in ten runs and hit five extra-base hits in the same game.
  • Dodger to bat in ten runs in the same game.

  • MLB player since RBI have been tracked to have:

    • hit three home runs in one game,
    • stolen two bases in one game,
    • batted in ten runs in one game,
    • hit five extra-base hits in one game, and
    • gotten six hits in one game

    across an entire career. And he did it all in one game.

Ohtani ended the season with 54 home runs and 59 stolen bases, and he was only caught stealing four times that entire season. In fact, he isn’t just the only member of MLB’s 50–50 club. He’s also the only member of its 45–45 club. Let’s break down how astonishing this accomplishment is:

  • Just two other players have hit at least fifty home runs and stolen at least fifty bases in separate years:

    1. Barry Bonds (52 stolen bases in 1990, 73 home runs in 2001)
    2. Brady Anderson (53 home runs in 1992, 50 home runs in 1996)

    Which makes for a great trivia question: “Who is the only Major League Baseball player besides Barry Bonds and Shōhei Ohtani to have hit at least fifty home runs and stolen at least fifty bases in any (not necessarily the same) season?” (I might’ve guessed the late, dearly lamented Rickey Henderson, who hit a respectable 297 career home runs, but never more than 28 in a season [1986, 1990].)

  • The 40–40 club has only five other members, and no one, including Ohtani, has entered it twice:

    • Two hit at least forty home runs the same year they stole at least forty-five bases:

      1. Ronald Acuña Jr. (2023: 41 home runs, 73 stolen bases)
      2. Alex Rodriguez (1998: 42 home runs, 46 stolen bases)

    • One hit at least forty-five home runs the same year he stole at least forty bases:

      1. Alfonso Soriano (2006: 46 home runs, 41 stolen bases)

    • The other two hit forty-two home runs the same year they stole forty bases:

      1. José Canseco (1988)
      2. Barry Bonds (1992)

    (Canseco, the first to do so, correctly predicted that he would accomplish the feat in 1988; ironically, he mistakenly assumed he would be the sixth or seventh player to do so. Mickey Mantle was subsequently quoted as saying, “Hell, if I’d known 40–40 was going to be a big deal, I’d have done it every year!”)

    Icing on the cake: Ohtani got to 40–40 in 21 fewer games than any of his predecessors. And he did it with a walk-off grand slam.

  • Of the 30–30 club’s fifty-one members, ten players qualified twice, two (Howard Johnson and José Ramirez) qualified three times, one (Soriano) qualified four times, and two (Barry Bonds and his father Bobby) qualified five times, for a total of seventy-nine.

  • Perhaps most importantly, Ohtani founded the 50–50 club while probably dozens of MLB pitchers who were in the midst of rehabbing from Tommy John surgery. One of those pitchers is named Shohei Ohtani. Because, as most people who read this were already aware, I may have deliberately buried the lede a bit.

    We’re obviously going to focus on that more, but first, I want to emphasize that this is what he did while he was injured. Just mind-boggling.

Back to top · Table of contents · My portfolio · Contact me · Website index

50–50 Club 2: Electric Boogaloo, or, a greater two-way player than Babe Ruth

Ohtani isn’t just a speed demon who can hit to all corners of the field. He also pitches a bit. If you’re European, imagine Pelé and Lev Yashin being the same player and you’ll get an idea of how rare this is. (Substitute David Beckham and Oliver Kahn if you’re a bit younger, and Lionel Messi and Manuel Neuer if you’re a lot younger.)

Ohtani is the first player to have excelled to this degree at both hitting and pitching since Hall of Famer Martín Dihigo retired from the Negro Leagues in 1945. He’s also by far the most famous player to have done so since one George Herman ‘Babe’ Ruth, and Ohtani has arguably already outdone the Bambino on numerous counts. Babe was an excellent pitcher and an incredible hitter, but he mostly focused on pitching with the Red Sox and on hitting with the Yankees; he was also a middling baserunner who got worse as he aged. Ohtani has both hit and pitched fairly consistently despite some injuries, and as we’ve seen, he’s also a superb baserunner.

On 2025-09-16, Ohtani founded another, even more remarkable 50–50 club, becoming MLB’s first player to strike out 50 batters and hit 50 home runs in the same season. (Bonus: He passed both milestones in the same game – and pitched five no-hit innings at that. Remarkably, Los Angeles’ bullpen somehow lost the game anyway.)

He’s also the first modern player to lead his league in pitching and offensive statistics in one season (2022): as a pitcher, he led the AL in strikeouts per innings pitched (11.87); as a hitter, he had the highest exit-velocity home run that season (111.9 mph); and as a baserunner, he led MLB with a home-to-first average time of 4.09 seconds. And 2022 was his weakest season since he spent most of 2020 recovering from an injury!

Needless to say, he’s won countless awards, including but hardly limited to:

  • 5x MLB All-Star (2021-2025)
  • 4x Silver Slugger Award at Designated Hitter (2021, 2023–2025)
  • 4x Most Valuable Player (American League 2021 & 2023, National League 2024 & 2025)
  • AL Rookie of the Year (2018)
  • NL Championship Series MVP (2025)

He’s only the second player to have been named MVP in both leagues (following Frank Robinson, who won the 1961 NL MVP as a Cincinnati Red and the 1966 AL MVP as a Baltimore Oriole). Ohtani won all four MVPs unanimously, which is unprecedented in at least two ways:

  1. In 2023, he became the first player to be unanimously voted MVP more than once.
  2. In 2025, he became the first player to win multiple MVPs in each league.

He’s also led either the AL or NL (and sometimes both) in several important statistics in up to three seasons since 2021 (his first full MLB season with no major injuries):

Shōhei Ohtani’s first-place season statistics

MLBAL/NL20212022202320242025

Clearly, his 2022 season lagged behind the other four, and of those four, note that the two seasons he led in IBB were the two he didn’t lead in home runs. This is not coincidental.

But is Ohtani a good enough pitcher to win a Cy Young Award someday? Perhaps. Let’s compare his last five years to Chris Sale’s. (Remember, in 2024, Sale won the Triple Crown and the NL Cy Young Award.)

Not all their stats are that similar, but it’s kind of spooky how close these are. If I saw those numbers devoid of context, I’d probably assume they were different five-year spans from the same pitcher.

Total plays: Ohtani’s unusually large impact as a two-way player

Baseball is effectively a series of pitcher-hitter interactions. Since the National League adopted the designated hitter in 2022, most players have only had chances to do one or the other. Coincidentally, in 2022, Ohtani faced 666 batters (BF) and made 660 plate appearances (PA). Neither of those is especially noteworthy on its own, but that sums up to a career-high 1,326 chances to affect a game’s score, which, for lack of a better term, I’ll just call total plays (TP). By contrast, the Miami Marlins’ Sandy Alcantara led MLB that year in BF (886), and the Texas Rangers’ Marcus Semien led MLB that year in PA (724). With both leagues now using the DH and no other pitchers batting for themselves, Ohtani led MLB in TP by 450.

So, how remarkable are 1,326 TP? Well, Jimmy Rollins’ 778 PA in 2007 are the single-season record, so you’ll have to pitch a lot to exceed 1,326 TP. The last player to do so was Steve Carlton in 1980, and narrowly at that: 1,221 BF, 111 PA, 1,332 TP. (He was also on fire as a pitcher, leading MLB with 162 ERA+, 2.42 FIP, 286 strikeouts, and 10.2 pWAR. Unsurprisingly, he won 1980’s NL Cy Young Award. His hitting stats, though, were .188/.190/.198, for −0.1 nWAR – respectable stats for a pitcher, but nothing to write home about. He still led MLB in overall WAR with 10.1; runner-up George Brett had 9.4.)

Legendary Atlanta Braves knuckleball pitcher Phil Niekro exceeded them both in 1979 far more comfortably, with 1,570 TP from 134 PA (with a respectable 0.4 nWAR; his .195/.213/.244 hitting stats are respectable by pitchers’ standards and actually almost legendary by 39-year-old pitchers’ standards) and 1,436 BF (with an MLB-best 7.4 pWAR); his 7.7 WAR was good for fifth-best in MLB. (In 1978, he’d led MLB in WAR outright with 10.4, outstripping runner-up [and AL Cy Young winner] Ron Guidry by 0.8. Since knuckleballs are unusually slow, knuckleballers often have unusually long careers; Niekro played his last MLB game at forty-eight, making him MLB’s tenth-oldest player ever. His career total of 22,677 BF is a modern record; all-time, only Cy Young [29,565 in 1890–1911], Pud Galvin [25,415 in 1875–1892], and Walter Johnson [23,415 in 1907–1927] faced more.)

Carlton again came close to exceeding 1,326 TP in 1982 (1,302 TP, 1,193 BF, 109 PA) and 1983 (1,288 TP, 1,183 BF, 105 PA), but that’s all, and since 1980, only a few other players have even come within 100:

  1. In 1982, Fernando Valenzuela’s 1,266 (1,156 BF, 110 PA) comes within 60 of Ohtani’s mark. His other highest totals were 1987 (1,222 TP, 1,116 BF, 106 PA) and 1986 (1,218 TP, 1,102 BF, 116 PA). His 116 PA are the most by any pitcher not named “Shōhei Ohtani” in any season since 1980.
  2. In 1980, Steve “Not Captain America” Rogers had 1,257 (1,151 BF, 106 PA), 69 behind Ohtani’s mark. (Nice.) The remarkably consistent Rogers came within 100 of Ohtani twice more, with 1,230 (1,122 BF, 108 PA) in 1982 and 1,229 (1,125 BF, 104 PA) in 1983.
  3. In 1985, Joaquín Andújar had 1,233 (1,127 BF, 106 PA), missing Ohtani’s mark by 93.
  4. In 1987, Charlie Hough (not coincidentally, also a knuckleballer) led MLB in BF (1,231), but since the AL used a DH, he did not bat at all, putting him 95 TP from Ohtani.

Naturally, Ohtani’s 45.5 WAR from 2021–2025 led MLB; only Aaron Judge (41.8) even came close, with Juan Soto (32.2) a distant third. Is it any surprise that Ohtani has been a unanimous MVP for three straight years?

August 31, 2022: Ohtani whiffs on a pitch so filthy he steals (and improves) it

On 2022-08-31, Ohtani faced Yankees closer Clay Holmes in the bottom of the eighth inning and struck out on a 100 mph sinker. The replay clearly shows how impressed he was. He’d been experimenting with a sinker since May that year and had gotten its velocity up to 100 mph, but it didn’t really have the sink a good sinker needs, nor did he have the command he wanted over it. According to his teammate Patrick Sandoval, Ohtani went to the bullpen the very next day, pulled up Holmes’ movement numbers, and went to town trying to match them.

Ohtani next pitched on 2022-09-03 against the Houston Astros. He struck out five batters in eight innings, giving up a single run on six hits. After a 100 mph sinker sailed by Chas McCormick for strike three, McCormick showed the same expression that Ohtani had shown after Holmes’ sinker. Ohtani threw his retooled sinker twenty times that day. The results are clear. (“VB” and “HB” respectively stand for ”Vertical Break” and “Horizontal Break”.)

Sinker Comparison
PitcherBatterDatemphrpmVBHB
OhtaniBlue Jays2022-08-2797.119355”15”
HolmesOhtani2022-08-3199.722154”18”
OhtaniMcCormick2022-09-0399.720928”20”

As of 2022-09-02, Austin Warren’s sinker had the highest horizontal break in MLB with 19.3”. Ohtani’s was 20”. Furthermore, of the top twenty horizontal-breaking sinkers in MLB, Anthony Bender’s had the highest velocity at 96.6 mph. Ohtani’s was 99.7. In short, he took a single bullpen session to learn to throw a new pitch better than everyone else in Major League Baseball.

Who else does anything even remotely like this? 大谷翔平が一角獣と実際のアニメの登場人物アはだ。(English felt inadequate to the occasion, so that’s my questionably grammatical Japanese for “Shōhei Ohtani is a unicorn and a real-life anime character.” Rōmaji: Ōtani Shōhei ga ikkakujū to jissai no anime no tōjō jinbutsu wa da.) I’d call him the real-life Son Goku, but his intellect ranges far wider than Goku’s ever did. Son Gohan, meanwhile, only unlocks his full power when he flies into a rage, and I’ve never seen Ohtani lose his cool. Then again, I’d almost wager money on him going Super Saiyan if he ever did.

2025 NLCS Game 4: The greatest single-game performance in athletic history

And, again, injuries have curtailed his pitching over his time in MLB; who knows how much better he’ll get if he avoids them. He might’ve given us a preview in 2025’s NL Championship Series’ fourth and final game, wherein he went where no one except callow adolescents ever even imagined going before. In that game, Ohtani:

  1. Became the first player to hit three home runs and strike out ten batters in one MLB game.
  2. Became the first pitcher ever to hit a leadoff home run in any postseason game.
  3. Became the first pitcher to hit multiple home runs in one postseason game.
  4. Became the first pitcher to clinch a World Series allowing no runs and at most two hits.
  5. Became only the second pitcher to hit three home runs in any MLB game (following Jim Tobin; see below).
  6. Became only the second pitcher to win any postseason series allowing no runs and at most two hits. (Justin Verlander, himself a modern legend, was the first, in a 2013 AL Division Series.)
  7. Became only the twelfth player ever to hit three home runs in one postseason game.
  8. Hit a home run that cleared the stadium (469 feet).
  9. Hit that night’s three hardest balls (all at least 113.6 mph).
  10. Threw its eleven fastest pitches (all at least 99.2 mph).
  11. Hit more home runs of at least 440 feet (2) than he’d given up in 102 career pitching appearances (1).
  12. Hit more home runs (3) than he gave up hits (2).
  13. Hit the only three home runs of the night.

  14. His twelve total bases in that game ties for seventh-highest in any playoff game in history:

    Most Total Bases in One Playoff Game
    #PlayerGameTB
    1. Bob Robertson 1971 NLCS G2 14
    Albert Pujols 2011 WS G3
    3. Adam Kennedy 2002 ALCS G5 13
    Hideki Matsui 2004 ALCS G3
    Pablo Sandoval 2012 WS G1
    Chris Taylor 2021 NLCS G5
    7. Babe Ruth 1926 WS G4 12
    Babe Ruth 1928 WS G4
    Reggie Jackson 1977 WS G6
    George Brett 1978 ALCS G3
    Adrian Beltré 2011 ALDS G4
    Jose Altuve 2017 ALDS G1
    Kiké Hernández 2017 NLCS G5
    Eddie Rosario 2021 NLCS G4
    Daulton Varsho 2025 ALDS G2
    Shohei Ohtani 2025 NLCS G4
    Shohei Ohtani 2025 WS G3

    (And, of course, I’ve gotten ahead of myself – we’ll discuss World Series Game 3 below.)

Numerous sportswriters immediately proclaimed it the greatest single-game performance by a single athlete in not just baseball history, but sports history. In short, it’s hardly a hot take to call Ohtani an athlete of historically unprecedented talent and skill.

Precedents to Ohtani’s 2025 NLCS Game 4

Ohtani’s NLCS Game 4 isn’t the only feat of its kind in baseball history, but it arguably still stands out from the others. I read a comment from someone to the extent that they’d had a player strike out ten batters and hit three home runs in MLB: The Show a few days before NLCS Game 4, and they’d chuckled to themselves about how unrealistic a feat it was. But it wasn’t unrealistic for Ohtani, since he’d already had a ten-strikeout game in which he’d hit two homers on 2023-06-27. There are several reasons NLCS Game 4 is more impressive: it was a postseason game, it featured one more home run, and Ohtani was more dominant on the mound – in the earlier game, he’d allowed one run on four hits. Until Ohtani, no one had ever struck out at least ten batters and hit at least two home runs in two games, and only five pitchers before him had even done it in one game:

  1. Milt Pappas (1961-08-27)
  2. Pedro Ramos (1963-07-31 G2)
  3. Rick Wise (1971-06-23) – the most impressive outing of the lot, as we’ll see in a moment
  4. Madison Bumgarner (2017-04-02)
  5. Zack Greinke (2019-04-02)

(Fun fact: Greinke was the starting pitcher of the game in which Bumgarner hit two home runs, and despite the latter’s efforts, he didn’t get the win – poor run support from his teammates and a bullpen collapse contributed to San Francisco losing the game. On the other hand, since it was an Opening Day game, and Bumgarner went 2 for 2 with a walk, he finished the day with 1.000/1.000/4.000 hitting stats for the season.)

A few slugging accomplishments by pitchers deserve further attention:

  • Until Ohtani, the Boston Braves’ Jim Tobin was the only pitcher who’d ever hit three home runs in one game, in a 6–5 win over the Chicago Cubs on 1942-05-13. Now technically, Ohtani only pitched six innings and was officially considered the designated hitter when he hit his third homer, while Tobin pitched a complete game. But then, Tobin committed a fielding error and allowed three earned runs on five hits, while Ohtani committed no fielding errors and allowed no runs on two hits.

  • The Atlanta Braves’ Tony Cloninger hit two grand slams in a 17–3 blowout against the San Francisco Giants on 1966-07-03. That remains the franchise record and ties with twelve other players for the MLB record. His nine RBI in a single game are also the franchise record.

    (Famously, the Braves made their pitchers take batting practice, so they were rarely guaranteed outs.)

  • On 1967-10-12, the St. Louis Cardinals’ Bob Gibson, arguably still amongst history’s most underrated pitchers, struck out ten batters and hit a home run in a 7–2 World Series-clinching win over the Boston Red Sox. To be fair, the World Series is even higher-stakes than the NLCS. But then, one home run isn’t three.
  • On 1968-05-08, James Augustus “Catfish” Hunter threw a 4–0 perfect game against the Minnesota Twins and went 3 for 4 at the plate with a double and three RBI. He also fanned eleven batters. But five total bases are a lot fewer than twelve, and again, the stakes were lower.
  • On 1971-06-23, the Philadelphia Phillies’ Rick Wise hit two home runs and threw a 4–0 no-hitter against a Cincinnati Reds lineup that included Pete Rose, Lee May, Tommy Helms, George Foster, and Hall of Famers Tony Pérez and Johnny Bench. But it wasn’t in a playoff game.

I intend in no way to diminish any of the above achievements, nor Ohtani’s achievements in the NLCS Game 4. However, although Tobin hit well enough to be used occasionally as a pinch hitter (in fact, he’d hit a pinch-hit home run literally the day before his three-home run game), none of the above pitchers ever hit on Ohtani’s level (and only Gibson pitched on it). Put another way, Ohtani’s second-best game is so much better than Wise, Cloninger, Tobin, or even Gibson’s second-best games (to the extent that… what even is Ohtani’s second-best game? There’s plenty of room for debate) that it’s not even really worth comparing them.

There’s also the matter of stakes. Ohtani’s game clinched the pennant against the team with the best record in baseball. Of the games above, only Gibson’s game had comparable stakes, since it was in the World Series.

One final accomplishment that might be worth considering: Don Larsen’s perfect game in the 1956 World Series, which is another accomplishment with comparable stakes. I never expect to see another World Series perfect game as long as I live (then again, Yoshinobu Yamamoto might surprise us someday). That said, Larsen might’ve pitched better, but he went 0 for 2 at the plate that day, so was it really a perfect game?

OK, I’m being somewhat facetious, but I’m making a serious point. Ohtani is performing at an elite level as both a hitter and a pitcher to an extent that was previously considered impossible. A new rule allowing starting pitchers to become designated hitters after being removed as pitchers is informally called “the Ohtani rule”. That speaks volumes: what other pitchers ever hit well enough for that to even be a consideration? We’ve seen better pitchers than Ohtani, better hitters than Ohtani, better baserunners than Ohtani, but no one else has done even the first two at such an elite level, much less all three.

And this is so historic – and it’s so easy to explain why it’s historic – that even people who never cared about baseball before are taking notice, much like Michael Jordan, Michael Phelps, Tiger Woods, and the Williams sisters had broad appeals that extended far beyond the usual basketball, swimming, golf, and tennis aficionados. Plus, it helps that Ohtani is just immensely fun to watch, with a joy for the game that’s simply infectious.

Back to top · Table of contents · My portfolio · Contact me · Website index

Placing Ohtani’s achievements in context

LeBron James, one of the greatest and most versatile basketball players of all time, has never finished a season in the top ten in all three of the sport’s most important statistics: points, rebounds, and assists. (In fact, although he is the career rebound leader among active players, he has never finished a season in the top ten.) Neither did Michael Jordan, who led the NBA in points year after year and remains its all-time leader in points per game, but was only a middling rebounder. It’s difficult to make 1:1 comparisons to baseball, but in 2022, Ohtani finished top ten in three major offensive statistics (4th in home runs, 6th in slugging%, 6th in OPS) and first or second place in three major pitching statistics (1st in SO/9IP, 2nd in pitching WAR, 2nd in FIP).

Back to top · Table of contents · My portfolio · Contact me · Website index

Other two-way players

Quick. Name the only player in Major League Baseball history to hit at least 500 home runs and post a career ERA under 2.00 and ERA+ above 250.

…

Sorry, “Babe Ruth” is not the correct answer. His career ERA is 2.28, and his career ERA+ is 122. Those are respectable numbers, but I will print the correct answer below the next table.

Most people seem to think Shōhei Ohtani and the Bambino are the only two-way players in major-league history. Most people are egregiously wrong. In over 150 years of American professional baseball, dozens of players have achieved major-league success as both hitters and pitchers; at least eight two-way players are Hall of Famers. (Arguably, nine are, and one of them – the correct answer to our above trivia question, as it turns out – will surprise you.) Before we meet some of them, a few notes:

  • “PG” means “Pitcher Games”, and “PWSS” means “Pitching WAR per Starter’s Season” (200 innings pitched).
  • “OG” means “Other Games”, and “NWFS” means “Non-Pitching WAR per Full Season” (162 games).
  • I’ve chosen to list players that meet at least one of two definitions:
    1. MLB’s official definition of two-way play, which requires a player, in a single season, to have:
      1. at least twenty innings pitched
      2. at least three plate appearances in at least twenty games as a position player or designated hitter
    2. Across an entire career, pitch in at least fifty games and play other positions in at least fifty games
  • For players who aren’t Hall of Famers, I also required at least 0.8 PWSS and 0.8 NWFS.
  • I’ve italicized players who achieved at least 1.6 NWFS and 1.6 PWSS and written their names in small caps.
  • I’ve also bolded Hall of Famers (I know Ohtani technically isn’t an inductee yet, but I’m pretty sure his athletic ability is inexplicable unless he possesses the power to manipulate time, so if anyone can get inducted into the Hall of Fame before their career even ends, it’s Shōhei Ohtani.)

  • Since a replacement-level player in 1871 and a replacement-level player in 2025 are playing at vastly different skill levels, we cannot assume wins above replacement to remain constant over time. Sabermetrician Bill James notes that “the best players were further from the average than they are now.” James may in turn be borrowing from evolutionary biologist Stephen Jay Gould’s argument in Full House: The Spread of Excellence from Plato to Darwin that .400 batting averages’ disappearance is a sign of a decline not in hitting quality, but in player skill levels’ variance. Gould notes that league averages have stayed roughly constant over time; what’s decreased is the standard deviation of individual averages, and the largest outliers alongside them.

    If anything, I’d argue that this is a sign that modern hitting and pitching are both much better. I’ll explain in detail below (“Even Ted Williams and Josh Gibson couldn’t hit .400 against modern rotations”), but the short version is: Williams and Ty Cobb never faced Bill Foster or Bullet Rogan; Gibson and Oscar Charleston never faced Bob Feller or Walter Johnson; and all eight players’ numbers improved as a result.
